Life

Sorcerer

— Zhang Xianting

We rely entirely on ghosts and spirits of the dead to live. Although whether ghosts and spirits truly exist in this world, I still cannot answer with certainty. But at the very least, people must believe in ghosts and spirits. Perhaps in foreign countries, in large cities, in places of civilization, there are very few people who live as we do? But in our backward, lowly rural society, our profession of seeking divine guidance, subduing demons, and delivering the deceased has an immense role. In a feudal society where ghosts and spirits control half of human affairs, people like us are precisely needed.

In other places, our names may be different, but here we are called "Duangong" or "Daoshi," meaning sorcerers. Besides occasionally serving as musicians for joyous celebrations such as weddings and birthdays, our main duties as Duangong Daoshi are to capture ghosts, deliver souls, and seek divine aid. Of course, some Duangong also practice old operas, performing at "temple fairs," "god gatherings," and "festivals" in their hometowns, or during people's "red and white affairs" (weddings and funerals). Some Daoshi also study "Yinyang," looking at the geography for the dead, but these are not the main tasks of our profession.

To earn a living in our profession is certainly not easy. I remember when I was ten years old and barely literate, I began preparing. Before officially becoming an apprentice, I had to read the preliminary scriptures at home, such as the "Earth Treasury Sutra," the "Sutra of Resolving Grievances," and the "Blood Basin Sutra," all read around the age of ten. I was already twelve years old when I became an apprentice. Being a Duangong is similar to being a doctor, a refined profession that requires some literacy. Not only must you be able to recite many fragmented scriptures, but you must also understand the formal documents that reach the Jade Emperor, know the proclamations to eliminate demons and monsters, and be able to write the yellow notices. You must even write elegies for the bereaved family, couplets of joy, longevity, and mourning for the host, and write invitations and funeral announcements for people. In my opinion, our talent and learning are generally more complex than what doctors possess. Naturally, in the eyes of the villagers, and in terms of rewards, we are better than doctors, that goes without saying. At my teacher's house, I read scriptures for another two years. During these two years, besides following the teacher to people's homes to "perform Dao rituals," "do small send-offs," and "celebrate altars," I also learned to write memorials and yellow notices at home. Usually, two-thirds of the time was spent going out with the teacher to make a living, so the time spent studying at home was very little. Therefore, many spells, divination methods, and various chants and Taoist practices were learned through practice. Moreover, this was not directly taught by the teacher, but learned by imitating the teacher's performances. In the third year, after thanking the teacher and performing a celebration, I was considered to have completed my apprenticeship.

When people get sick, we get business. Villagers believe that illnesses are caused by ghosts and spirits, either from past grievances or offending the heavens. They need us to do the work of sending away ghosts, eliminating disasters, and resolving grievances. This is called "Da Xiaosong" (small send-off), also known as "Da Luo" (beating the gong), which means sending away ghosts. "Da Luo" involves inviting a dozen or so Duangong, beating large gongs and drums, making a big commotion in the patient's home. Using peach wood talismans and command arrows (fake bows and arrows made of peach branches), they shoot randomly at the patient's door and bed, and various talismans are pasted on the doors, so that ghosts and monsters dare not enter again. People are dressed as ghosts and demons, and then disguised as divine soldiers to shoot, kill, and subdue them. Before doing these things, the patient's birth date and time must be written on a document and burned to report to the heavenly court. Then, the patient's illness and offending stars are investigated through divination, and the method of sending them away is determined. The "Da Luo" event starts at two in the afternoon and continues until the next morning. A boat made of rice straw is also made for the patient and burned with paper candles, called "sending the boat," meaning to send the ghosts away. A bowl of cold rice and some water are also scattered in the air near the house along with the paper candles, which is called "rewarding water rice," to let the ghosts eat. In short, both forceful and gentle methods are used to deal with ghosts. One is to capture and drive them away, and the other is to buy them off and respectfully send them away. This depends on what kind of ghost is offended. Wealthy families mostly beat gongs to catch ghosts, while poor families mostly "Da Xiaosong" to send ghosts away. Perhaps it is because wealthy people have power, and ghosts are afraid of them; while poor people have no money, and ghosts bully them?

The small send-off also requires returning after midnight. According to the rules, we cannot stay overnight in the host's house. No matter how late it is, how far the journey is, or how bad the weather is, you must go home to rest. In the severe winter with snowy weather, in the middle of the night, we often have to walk twenty or thirty miles in the mountains with a small lantern. The body is extremely tired on the road. The whole day is spent singing, doing hand gestures, and walking. The whole day is spent breathing in incense, paper ash, and oil fumes. Your head is already dizzy, and you still have to stumble through the snow, braving the wind and rain, to walk this dark, lonely, and rugged mountain path. Naturally, we are the ones who capture and send away ghosts, and we eat ghost rice all day long. But when you are on this desolate and uninhabited high mountain, in this place filled with graves, can you not be frightened, afraid that demons will harm you and take revenge on you?

Every family in the village has an altar for gods and Bodhisattvas. The altar gods are specifically in charge of the life, death, and diseases of cattle, horses, and livestock. The farmers' hopes throughout the year are "abundant harvests" and "thriving livestock." Therefore, every year there is a "small celebration" for the altar gods who govern livestock, and every three years there is a "big celebration." Small celebrations only require inviting two or three Duangong. Big celebrations require a dozen or so Duangong. It also lasts from two in the afternoon until dawn the next day. Celebrating the altar is entirely a celebration, and it also includes some nonsensical dramas and songs. During the celebration, a pig must be killed, covered with incense and candles, and offered before the altar gods. We love the business of celebrating the altar, partly because the work is easy and enjoyable, and partly because the rewards are rich. In addition to a peck or two of fragrant rice and one to three chickens and ducks, you can earn more than ten thousand copper coins per person. But there is also one of the most cruel things: bloodletting. The Duangong must use the sharp tip of the knife used to kill the pig to prick his own head, draw blood and drip it into a wine glass, mix it with wine, and sprinkle it in the cattle pen and pigsty. In this way, the livestock will not be infected with the plague and will grow "uniformly." The procedure of bloodletting is mostly done by apprentices. Although the teachers receive rich fragrant rice and profits, they are unwilling to shed blood. Before bloodletting, a piece of hair must be shaved off the head, the top of the head is warmed with hot paper ash, and a few cups of hot wine are drunk. Then, one kowtows and prostrates before the altar gods, silently chanting incantations, before using the tip of the knife to prick the head. I had never drawn blood before, and I was not very afraid when I first heard my teacher's comfort, saying that if you are pious in your heart, it won't hurt at all. But after the second and third times, almost once a month, I felt very frightened. No matter how I prayed, how sincere I was, the knife was unbearably painful when it pricked my head. My hand holding the knife trembled constantly, but for the sake of money and the teacher's orders, I had to sacrifice. We Duangong fear this kind of "surgery" and bloodletting work.

Another of our duties is to perform "Dao rituals." Some are for temple fairs to deliver wandering souls of the past, doing "water and land Dao rituals," "reciting yellow scriptures," and "performing clear singing," but most are to "do meritorious deeds" for the deceased of a certain family. When the deceased has just breathed their last, the Duangong holds a "leading banner" and sings in front of the deceased, which is called "opening the road," to guide the deceased to the underworld. After opening the road, there is "reciting scriptures," "sending the funeral," and so on. If the deceased is a woman, then there must be "breaking the prison," opening hell and rescuing the deceased from hell. This imitates the story of Mulian rescuing his mother. Women must have deep sins and be beaten into "Avici Hell."

Our kind of life is generally very bitter. At night, we are afraid of being disturbed by ghosts and cannot sleep, and we also have to "operate" and draw blood. But when we encounter virtuous hosts, their treatment is very generous. There are three or four meals of wine and food every day, tea and snacks, and fragrant rice and profits when leaving. Afterwards, when we meet in the street market, they still invite you to drink tea and wine, and you are also quite respected. However, if you are "Da Xiaosong" for someone, and the deceased happens to die at this time, then the patient's family will be sorry for you, thinking that your Taoist skills are not high and cannot drive away evil. They will immediately drive you out, and naturally you will not receive any reward. Now the countryside is even more impoverished. There are still people doing small send-offs, but there are fewer families beating gongs; there are still small celebrations, but there are fewer big celebrations; some people only open the road when someone dies, and rarely do Dao rituals, and some even don't even open the road because too many people have died. Therefore, our business is gradually declining.
